Chapter on: When a fracture occurs in the head.
The types of fractures are multiple, and the shapes of them are diverse, and the causes are distinct, since some fractures are caused by blows to the bone, and the blow completely breaks the bone from within, which is called comminuted; or the blow cuts a part of the bone and the wound cuts the flesh but does not pass through to the other side; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound in these two types of fractures is either large or small, and whether the fracture is contracted or traction; and its cause is a blow with a hammer or by falling on stones, and similar to them.
And this fracture is penetrating, which approaches the panniculus that is behind the bone; or it is on the face of the bone, and the wound in these two types of fractures can be large or small.
And there is a type of fracture that is weakened in the bone, in the fineness of the hair-like fissures, and for this reason this type of fracture is called capillary.
A fracture that is caused by falling or hitting a stone, and similar, which causes one layer of the bone to split at the joints from within, and it occurs in concave areas such as when it happens to the kneecaps when they are hit and split from the inside.
And moreover, this occurs in bones that are soft, such as infant skulls.
And to all these types of fractures, they are like scattered stars. I will return to the memory of everything in its place.
And we should know all these types of fracture by detection on them and investigation of them with tests and the examination of rotten flesh that is over them.
A special type of capillary is recognized because it appears on the bone, is drained, and coagulates over it when it becomes black, as a fracture appears.
In the treatment of fractures, one should first consider the patient's symptoms, and if you find signs that indicate fear such as vomiting of bile, extension, loss of consciousness, abscess of the throat, fainting, acute fever, inflammation or redness of the joints and similar symptoms in the larger context, one doubts whether it will heal.
And if you find unusual symptoms, you hope for health, then you begin treatment of it.
The cause is that if it has come to you to treat it at the beginning as it comes in winter days, then it is necessary for you to study the bone structure before the twelfth day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you take care of the bone before the seventh day, knowing that the bone will be rotten by the panniculus, and accidents may occur as we have advised.
And if the fracture has reached the panniculus covering the brain, and it is divided into small pieces and crushed, then it is necessary that you open up the compressed and fragmented part, as I will advise you.
And it is that you shave the head of the patient with the injury, and you open up on the bone according to your custom and as you can, following the figure of the wound, and be gentle with the patient.
And if it happens that you must examine the bone, discharge blood or an abscess, then you should proceed with what is necessary, and it is that you apply warm cloths soaked in wine and rose oil to the wound, and let it rest until the abscess subsides, and be sure of discharging blood; and begin with the examination of the bone and removal of it.
And that is one of two methods of operation.
And the first method is that you open up the bone with a straight saw-like instrument, and this is its form: Take another saw of similar size; and this is its form:
Then apply more the saw than the second.
It is necessary that you have a number of saws of different sizes, some of which are longer than others; and some of them should be shorter than the others, and they should be at the end of their sharpness, and made of iron or steel.
Use them with ease in striking the saw to avoid damaging the head and teeth.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you drill around it before administering the saws with handles, which are called non-penetrating chisels.
They are not called thus because they do not reach the end of the bone to that which is at the head, so that the chisel has a rounded or tapered tip on its sharp edge resembling the rim or small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the bone.
It is convenient that you take many of these chisels as needed for the thickness of each bone, until it is present at all parts of the skull where the edge is sharp, the quantity in length and width according to the thickness of that bone.
This is the form of three types of chisels: The quality of drilling around the broken bone is that you place the chisel on the bone and rotate it with your thumbs until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the chisel to another place, and place what is between all the way through the thickness of the ray or around it.
Then open up the bone with saws between each crack, and do it as finely as possible at the end of delicacy, as we have said, until you break the bone, or with your hand, or with any other cause using instruments that you will prepare for this, such as tweezers and forceps.
It is necessary that you be careful so that the incision does not touch any cause of the hernia.
When you perforate the bone, if it is continuous with the hernia and separated from it, and you have to extract that part, it is necessary to cut and round off that part on the edge of the bone using a tool similar to an incision, but nonetheless it is necessary that it be very sharp and very thin compared to other incision tools.
If any small fragment of the bones remains and adheres, then take it with ease using what you have of tools; and apply ointment and poultice to the wound which we have mentioned.
The other method of operation is easy and distant from fear, which we call guillotine and the name is praised greatly; and this is the meaning: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin to uncover the fracture part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most visible and manifest, until when you have discovered that part and placed a cushion under the end of the incision tool, which has the following form (long and curved):
It is true that the semi-lunar fracture is round and does not cut any cause, while the acute fracture is in two directions advancing in length, as sometimes; so that the semi-lunar fracture should be adjacent to the hernia with its rounded edge cutting into the bone.
Then strike the incision tool against one part of the bone with a small hammer until you have cut the entire bone with ease, and you should be confident in the padding that no accident will occur in any way; and if the patient were the most foolish man among all men and the most ignorant about medicine, certainly, he would be asleep.
Then, if any cause is attached to the bone of the padding in some locations of the bone, cut that part with the rounded tip of the incision tool, and remove it easily, as it has been freed from harm and fear.
If the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the padding, and what is detached or remains attached to the bone is the surface, and it remains rough and subtle protrusions, then you must cut that roughness and remove those subtle protrusions with sharp razors which you have prepared in various quantities so that it is possible for you to administer them in all locations as most conveniently and correctly according to where you lose your operation of the broken bone and its shape.
It is necessary that you use the razor with which you cut the bone at first on the most subtle part of it, and do not stop doing so until you reach the point of administering the very thin layer and the very subtle part of all of them.
But in any case of small vein-like fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what you lost at correction of it.
It is because it cannot be reduced in all those who have studied that art. Stand on what we have shown about large fractures.
If the padding is rectangular when you remove the bone, then you must take cloth of the same size as the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place that on the orifices of the wound; and take another cloth doubled or tripled and immerse it in vinegar and rose oil and place that over the first cloth lightly on top of it, so as not to injure the hernia; and apply ointment sparingly over it, but not forcefully except according to the size of the wound alone.
Leave it with this care for a day or two until you are sure of cautery abscess; and then remove that and administer medicines with which excessive dryness is required, such as lentils and flour, and powder of sandalwood and aristolochia, and similar ones.
It is that you make the powder of these medicines dry on the wound, as it dries; and generally, administer all medicine with properties of which it absorbs and does not injure.
Take care according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and do not apply ointment or any other substance; nor leave it with putridity or anything similar; for when it is adjacent to the padding of the brain, it corrupts that and then poisons the patient, causing him great evil.
If it happens in the hernia of the head, as the bone is purified and especially when the care of it is administered, and it turns black on its surface; then consider whether the patient has experienced the accidents that we have mentioned, know that it is harmful without any doubt.
And if it does not happen blackness except from the medicine that is applied, and it is due to that medicine that such blackness occurs, then you must take a part of honey and three parts of oil. and cover that area with a cloth, and place a cure for healing on it until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken in the nose but two fractures together with one of them observed, because there are two bones, and the other fracture is cartilaginous, and it does not break and will not happen to it except traction, curvature, and deviation.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then it must be that you insert your little finger into its own nose and grasp that fracture inside with your thumb, and with your index finger outside, until the nose returns to its natural figure. And let this be done for your convenience, and be careful not to hurt the patient during your treatment.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the little finger does not reach it, then it must be aligned with the end of the thumb where there is a small swelling.
If the fracture is in two parts, you should do what was said above.
And apply the restoration to it in the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then wait for the seventh or tenth days to rest the cauterized wound.
Insert a small piece of cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or two tweezers, if the fracture is in two parts, and it should be with the thickness of a sufficient amount to fill the cleft of the nose.
And as the ancient restorers said, the tweezers should be wrapped in thread and tied throughout the day, but I do not see this; however, it must be that the piece of cloth is wrapped in hog's fat with powdered millstone, and leave it in until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
Sometimes insert your fingers into the nose instead of tweezers, in case the little finger is not available because it is wrapped in lion's cloth; and it should be that you already have the fracture of the nose very strong and the patient is not prohibited from inspiration.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you want, do it, and if you want, use tweezers.
And if there is another wound cauterized, fill the nose with beeswax or cotton stuffed with vinegar oil or diacylon.
But if there is no cauterized wound, you must fill it outside with sieved flour and powdered charcoal mixed with hog's fat, and place a lion-skin cloth over it, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, you must cut it open, extract those bones using instruments suitable for them; and sew up the incision, heal it with what nourishes and consolidates, and use the appropriate filler.
If a wound occurs inside the nose, you must heal it with tweezers and administer leaden cannula until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, then examine it to see if the fracture is only on the outside, and whether it is not broken in two and curved towards the inside partitions, then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is in the right side, you must insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the right index finger; and press gently against the swelling of the fracture towards the inside partitions with the other hand from the outside, while pushing evenly on that area.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, you must administer extension of the two sides according to rectitude until it is possible for them to be aligned.
And if there is shaking of teeth to dislocation, then stabilize those that you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or ivory; and place a beeswax on the broken jaw, and place a double cloth, and place a large piece of felt well made, or one piece of sola the length of the jaw, over it, and place it appropriately depending on how the healing of it is prepared and what the application requires, until it is not destroyed.
And tell the patient to rest and take a calm diet, and stop giving them sour foods.
If you suspect that the figure has been altered in some way, then wait until the third day and remove it; afterwards, correct what has been altered and apply powder of milled oregano, album of egg white, or flour of camomile, as you will have left the wound open, and let it heal with woolen lamb's wool.
Keep that plaster attached to it and, if the bone's disposition is not altered, leave it alone until it is healed and the fracture is strengthened; for this fracture is usually tightened in three weeks.
And if another swelling appears as an abscess, administer what was mentioned earlier in its treatment until it is healed, and let the abscess be drained.
If the fracture is ulcerated, consider whether it is a separated piece of bone or shattered, then be careful to remove those shattered pieces with the appropriate instrument.
If the orifices of the ulcer are narrow, you should widen them with a blunt probe according to your need.
Then, when you have removed those shattered pieces and no pus has flowed out of them, widen the orifices of the ulcer if they are wide; otherwise, place one of the dressings suitable for this purpose on it and pack it, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is fractured.
Usually a broken clavicle breaks at the junction of the bone, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, separated, or separated into two pieces that do not shatter; and this is easy to restore; or it happens like this when the bone shatters, making restoration difficult; or the fracture occurs with an ulcer.
And the operation when the fracture is without an ulcer is that you have two assistants present; one will hold the broken clavicle and follow it, while the other extends the neck to that junction.
Then align the fracture with your thumbs until the bone shape is correct and neither protrude too much nor have a concavity.
And if you need more extension, cover the patient's hand with fabric or wool and let its size correspond to your need; extend, lift, and bend the clavicle and hold the hand with your hand until you align the fracture as required.
And if you cannot pull the extremity of the clavicle to the junctions outside, because it is prevented by the pronator, then have the patient lean back on their neck and place a larger cushion on the muscle of the affected side.
The assistant presses the muscle of the affected side towards the junctions until the clavicle bone is lifted beyond the pronator and then you correct the fracture and align it with your thumbs.
If you feel shattered bones in the clavicle and they are painful, then you must break it open and extract those shattered pieces with ease.
If it is held in the bone, you must make an incision in it with one of the instruments you have prepared for this purpose, as you had placed the clavicle chisel on the side that serves to chip; and this tool is a rod or iron with the following shape:
And its shape is like a spoon with this concavity; and let its width correspond to your need for it, according to the size of the bone or the thinness of it.
However, the length of it should be as much as possible for the operation; it must have two ends like this, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the ulcer that you have incised when you extracted the bone shatter is wide and it is certain that there is an abscess, then you adjust the two lips of the ulcer with sutures.
If the disruption is small or you fear an abscess, fill the ulcer with fabrics and powders according to the size of the ulcer's incision.
If an abscess occurs, pour wine with vinegar and oil on it and let it rest.
And it is necessary that the splint not be harmful to the broken bone; therefore, you must observe the patient every day, as when the limb becomes soft or the swelling goes down, then you correct it and strengthen the wound.
Then place the pillow of the patient on their neck and place the head of the patient on a small cushion; so that the neck of the affected limb may be raised, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by lifting the muscle; or the arm of the patient is tied to the neck of the affected limb.
And do not loosen the bindings unless there is pus or an abscess in that area, for up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see the face of it, and adjust in the tightness and restore it until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and made comfortable according to the month in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it is done in months of time for that purpose.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
If the shoulder blade is broken near its edge and not through it.
And when it is broken at that location, and if it is broken in the middle of it, but not through it, and it is not known except by touch; then according to the shape of the fracture you prepare for it and reduce it according to its natural shape in every possible way using any tools available to you.
Then you place powdered poplar bark mixed with alum and lion's wool on the site; and place a double layer of cloth, and then place the sharp stake of thin wood according to the size of the shoulder blade, or slightly more.
But if the stake is hollow in some of the locations of the shoulder blade, then you fill that concavity with lion's wool and place the stake according to its size.
And strengthen it above with long human hair and tying that is appropriate and the firmness of the stake until it is no longer swollen in its place.
And consider the bindings every day, and in all cases when it becomes soft, loosen it again and tighten it equally if it is swollen in its place.
Keep the patient on his side.
The shoulder blade is restored in twenty days or more.
And when that number is complete, then you loosen the bindings and ensure that you know for sure that it is not feared or threatened by the bones from which the fracture began, nor its disintegration.
If the bone has not passed and the pus has not subsided, you cut it off, remove it, and proceed as previously described for the treatment of a collarbone fracture with an abscess or cauterization if this occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
Sometimes the foot is broken in its proper place and it heals, but sometimes the extremities of it are crushed and pulverized.
And when the bone of the foot is broken in its middle, it causes a decline into the partitions within and leads to great pain, difficulty in walking, cough, high fever, and vomiting; and it is evident in the cavities of the broken bones and cannot be hidden from sensation.
The restoration is that the patient should be placed on his back and a cushion placed between his shoulders; then press the muscles of it, adjust the costal bones to its sides, and gently handle the operation with precision and accuracy according to every possible method and what is convenient, until the figure of the bone returns.
Then place the plaster and wadding on it, and place a lightweight wooden or hollow stick of similar nature on top of it, then you wrap that in cloth; and handle the broken bone gently so as not to harm it; and go along with the curvature of the backbone occasionally, strengthen it with appropriate compression, and consider the tension at all times, and when it is softened, strengthen it again.
And if there is the necessity to remove the plaster due to corrosion or pain, or an abscess that occurs in that place, then you cut and loosen it and remove the platter and correct anything caused by it with curative methods; and then adjust the plaster, if necessary, and apply appropriate compression until it heals.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are fractured.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in thick places where they are adjacent to each other; and at their extremities from the partition front, this is caused not by contraction but due to cartilage. And knowledge of this is not concealed from sensation, you must examine it with the questions.
And the restoration should be so that you guide the fracture with questions according to the possible method, until it is united as necessary, and then plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a stick, if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs declines into the partitions within, then the patient experiences intense pain and puncture, similar to puncture in those who have wept, and this is due to the bones being covered with linen; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is a difficult cure.
And the ancients were cunning in that with many schemes.
And they said that: it is necessary to place food for the patient that generates inflammation and wind, so that the abdomen is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed towards the partitions outside.
But we should avoid that for it not to be a light cause for generating dangerous abscesses, if not necessary; and if necessary, then help with it.
Some of them said that it should be placed on windy ground and that it should be squeezed with force; and this is similar to reasoning, but it is feared that the wind draws excess towards the place due to weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that is immersed in oil of myrrh, and powders placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the tension is guided when wrapping it according to curvature; and cure the patient with treatment for pleurisy and medicine.
And if a patient experiences intense pressure that he cannot bear and the bone is pierced with linen through puncture, you fear for him; then it is necessary to cut open the place, expose the broken rib, and place an instrument to preserve the cavity, the memory of which has been mentioned before, and to divide the bones easily and extract it.
And then adjust the two lips of the wound if it is large with stitching and treat it with plaster until it heals.
If there is an internal abscess, then you cut and pour powder in roasted oil on it and place a plaster over the place, and cure the patient with what is necessary for the internal abscess.
And be placed on a bed on which he sleeps, until it heals.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and they adhere to each other, and similar to the vertebrae of the back. And when something similar occurs and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider whether its limbs are already softened and stiffened and dead and cannot move nor strengthen them and when you constrict them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle and do not feel pain in them, then you know that it is not healed, and in the month of dispositions, it is fatal.
And if they move and feel constriction with fingers and piercing, then you know that the neck of the bones is saved and that the patient can recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck resembling something and you want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider the feet of it.

Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the fracture of the ankle bone.
The ankle bone is what is between the heel and the shoulder of the arm.
And if it is broken, the restoration of it is done in one of two ways, of which one is that you take flat pieces of moderately coarse millstone in this manner:
And place them at the two extremities of two pieces of linen, and then suspend them from a raised location.
Follow the patient over a chair, and place their broken arm on the millstone pieces until it reaches the handle in the appropriate location where its curve is attached to the millstone.
Suspend it from some heavy object, or else the servant extends it to the sides; and let the doctor and his assistant hold the broken bone with their hands until the fracture returns as necessary.
The other method is that the patient lies on their back and suspends their hand from the neck with a piece of linen; then command two servants, one of whom holds something over the fracture with his hands, and the other holds the swelling below it; each extends to their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then strengthen over the fracture with two pieces of linen, and on each side with another piece of linen; and each servant extends to their sides.
Therefore, if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then you must place a part of the linen on the handle and the other over the bone where it joins the heel.
In the same way, if the fracture is close to the heel, then you must place the linen over that location and over the heel.
Wrap it with ease and without effort, and then when it is tied as necessary and applied well, strengthen it if there is no abscess present.
If an abscess occurs, leave the wrapping for six days, and place on top of it linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess subsides; then strengthen it.
The method of strengthening it is to apply a plaster to the fracture, then cover it with linen wrapping over the plaster; and adjust the arm on that ankle bone, and place your hand under the muscle.
Cover the linen and strengthen the ankle bone with the arm so that the arm is in place, unless there is some reason for this due to the broken bone.
And use splints.
This is that you place over the fracture a splint that is wider and stronger than the others; and place between each splint the width of the finger, and the length of the splints according to the breakage with an addition of three fingers from all sides.
And then strengthen the splints with the bandage as described at the beginning of this chapter.
And that your bandage over the location of the fracture be tighter. And when the fracture is longer, the bandage should be looser.
And so if you see the position of the stars and the constriction as we said at the time when you are restoring the member to its proper position, then do you make (the procedure).
If it is a cauterized abscess, then you leave the constriction and the stars in for seven days as we said.
Then consider the ligament in all parts for three days to prevent inflammation or pressure at the site; or food is forbidden to reach the member due to the surplus of constriction; and so do everything as we said.
But if you are certain that there is no cause for this, then do not remove the ligature until three days or more.
Let the patient's bed be on their back, and let their hands be on their stomach.
Place a hot compress filled with wool on the fracture and consider at all times of night or day that the figure of the broken member must not be separated nor dissolved; or that the ligature is softened, so do everything as you see fit.
The patient's food should be according to the order we gave before, in such a way that the food is subtle at first and then becomes stronger; and so it is necessary to coarsen the food for them.
It is customary for the compress and blanket of him to be strong for forty days, and so it is necessary that they are removed and a bath is given, and he is treated with enemas which remedy that condition.
If the fracture is shallow, then do not remove the ligature and stitches until three days or two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones that are called two focal points; one of which is small and it is the one mentioned above near the elbow, and the other is large and it is the one placed below the small one, from the inner side of the elbow.
If only the larger bone (focal point) is broken or if both are fractured.
When the smaller bone (focal point) above is broken, the restoration of it is easier, and its healing is more rapid.
When the bone (focal point) below is broken, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
Worse still is when two bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller one above, it is appropriate for the physician to pause with a small extension in order to make the healing easier.
But if the large bone (focal point) is fractured, you must pause with a more forceful extension.
If both bones (focal points) are fractured, you must pause the reduction less forcefully.
You must pause the figure of the hand during the restoration and extension of it, with the palm facing upwards as we said before; the thumb should be positioned above the other fingers, as well as what has been said before; the earlobe should be lower than the others, and the patient should lie on a low couch.
There should be a pillow in front of him at the height or incline of his head to prevent him from exerting himself.
The assistant should extend the arm from above with his hand or ligature and another assistant should do the same from below in the same manner.
The physician should hold the bone until it is reduced according to the best shape possible.
If there are any shards of the bone, you should make a thorough reduction at the site with your skill.
If a piece of the bone appears separated that cannot be reunited and is not in your hope for its restoration, you should make an incision on it and remove it as we said before.
But if the fracture is with a wound, we have already given you special instructions on that matter, and so take care of it accordingly.
If an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, you should line a cloth with a mixture of rose oil and white wax that is neither too thick nor too thin; strengthen the cloth over the abscess with a gentle pressure using a subtle reduction until the abscess subsides; then remove the wax and apply an emulsion prepared from powdered moly and clover oil, and place stitches; the stitch that is placed over the fracture should be sharp and strong.
You should know that the number of stitches for an arm is determined by the major positioning (disposition), or whether the fracture is in one bone (focal point) or in two bones together.
You should pause your reduction over the site of the fracture with strong and forceful pressure; when it is necessary to extend to parts above or below the fracture, pause the reduction a little less firmly as we mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
The cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist; they should not be hard or rough, and the thread used for suturing should be of moderate thickness as we have recommended.
Consider the member and the ligature for several days. If there is any reason for which correction is necessary such as inflammation occurring in the member, you must suture the member with hot water until it subsides.
And you must release the limb until it relaxes again, and return the constriction.
And if the stiffness has been softened and the bone has returned to its normal state, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider more carefully if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction, for then you must soften it slightly and leave it for a few days until the nourishment returns, and apply heat to it.
But if the patient does not suffer from any of the causes mentioned earlier, then there is no need to fast for more than about twenty days or so around that time.
Then have the patient's hand suspended by its strength of movement, and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture heals in thirty days or thirty-two days, or it can heal more quickly in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the condition of the complexion and the application of your skill.
Chapter XIII
This is the title of the chapter. It translates to "On the healing of a finger fracture and dislocations."
Know that when a finger is fractured or the joints of the knuckles are dislocated, it does not heal without proper treatment.
And when there is a finger fracture or dislocation, the patient must follow a diet appropriate to his condition and rest it on a pillow in an extended position, with the hand of the affected limb resting on top of it.
Then apply an extender to the broken bones, and the doctor should align them correctly with a good fit.
You must apply plaster and dressing if there is no abscess, but if an abscess occurs, pierce it with a probe according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
And if the fracture is within the palm, you should hold the back of the hand and make a bandage of fabric, instructing the patient to hold their injured hand in place with their other hand; bind it tightly with long fabric.
The probe should be made of leather with some slack, so that the probe can be applied to the palm and bound according to necessity.
If the fracture is outside the palm, you must bind the probe and another probe from the injured area to the back of the hand, so that the hand remains open and straight; then bind the affected area as it would be when bound to the forearm with linen strips.
If there is a fracture in one of the knuckles, you should bind it as required if it is a push fracture, and hold it in place with the palm while binding it; if desired, use a small straight probe to elevate the fracture and prevent it from healing incorrectly.
If the fracture is in one of the other knuckles (such as the middle finger joint, index finger joint, ring finger joint, or little finger joint), bind it and apply the necessary splint to immobilize it; or use a continuous bandage for the entire length of the finger, which is better; or use a small probe on top of it, as described in the case of the push fracture.
In your healing process, also consider the appearance of an abscess and its opposite (opposition) using the appropriate procedure, as well as what is necessary when any of these occurrences happen, according to the narrative provided.
Chapter XIV
This is the title of the chapter. It translates to "On the healing of a hip bone fracture."
A broken and dislocated hipbone is identified by its appearance, which resembles the part above and above the pubic bone.
The restoration (of the patient) is done in such a way that the plaster (or bandage) is strengthened over the fracture and another plaster removed from the fracture, and the patient is prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then extend the entire plaster to its limit according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone. (Note: "liament" refers to the plaster or bandage used for healing.)
But if it is necessary to reduce the swelling of the thigh, then the plaster should be close to the edge at the end of the thigh, so that its extension covers it, and another plaster removed from the fracture. (Note: "pentenilh" refers to the thigh bone or femur.)
Similarly, if the fracture is close to the edge, then make the plaster close to the edge and extend it to the sides. (Note: This suggests that when the fracture is near the end of a bone, the plaster should be placed closer to the end and extended further out to provide support.)
Then the doctor should put both hands inside and reduce the bone according to its natural shape, so that it is joined well; at this point, you must stop plastering and applying tight bandages, unless there is an abscess in the limb. (Note: This suggests that the doctor should manipulate the broken bone back into its correct position before applying a plaster or bandage, but if there is an infection present, they should not apply the plaster or bandage to prevent further spread of the infection.) But if there is an abscess, leave it for several days until the abscess subsides, and then return to your care. (Note: This suggests that if there is an infection present, they should not apply the plaster or bandage immediately but wait for the infection to subside before continuing treatment.)
The bandaging should be done in this way: you must wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it over two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg and the swelling of the thigh. (Note: This suggests that the hard bandage should be tightly wrapped around the fracture site and the affected limb to provide support and prevent movement.)
Insert a long iron rod between the thigh and the leg near the edge on both sides, and bring the ends of the rod from above both sides, and then turn it around the leg and thigh so that any excess bandage wraps over it. (Note: This suggests that an iron rod should be inserted between the thigh and the leg to provide additional support during treatment.)
Place a splint on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place another splint over the bone of the leg. (Note: This suggests that a splint should be used to immobilize the affected limb during treatment.)
Fill the void between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the bleeding stops. (Note: This suggests that the affected area should be filled with soft cloths to stop any bleeding or oozing of fluid.)
Begin with a strong bandage over the location of the fracture with three or four layers, and as you approach the plaster at the site of the fracture, your bandaging should be less tight and more loose and moist. (Note: This suggests that the affected area should be tightly wrapped initially, but as the plaster approaches the fracture site, it should be loosened to allow for swelling and healing.)
Extend the iron rod to both ends of the leg on both sides as far as it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints of the other limb so that the plaster does not move from its position. (Note: This suggests that the affected limb should be immobilized with splints at both ends to prevent movement during treatment.)
Leave the bandaging in this way so that it does not cause corrosion, abscess, or inflammation and resembles those; if any of these occurrences happen, then remove the plaster immediately, and correct everything appropriately, according to what has been said previously. (Note: This suggests that the bandage should be applied in a way that does not cause infection or inflammation, and if any complications arise, they should be addressed immediately.)
But if there is a bone tumor present, you must remove it as much as possible; and if it cannot be completely removed, cut off the surrounding area, and treat with what was previously mentioned until it heals. (Note: This suggests that if there is a tumor in the bone, it should be removed as much as possible, but if complete removal is not possible, the affected area should be surgically excised and treated.)
Some weeks are stronger than this thigh fracture, as mentioned in the advice and in the arm. (Note: This suggests that the healing process for certain types of fractures, such as those in the thigh or arm, may take longer than others.)
However, in the restoration of this type, the patient should not experience limping; if it is only restored, and another limb is not applied, then limping is necessary for the one that is always used. (Note: This suggests that in some cases, a patient may need to use crutches or a walking aid on their unaffected leg to compensate for the immobilized affected limb during recovery.)
You should know that the illness [gout] lasts for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of complications and other dispositions.

Chapter XVII.
On the fracture of the heel and the bones of the foot and toes.
Not every heel suffers from fracture; but some bones of the foot suffer fracture; and in the toes only occasional fractures occur, and they do not occur except by excessive movement.
If the bones of the foot break and sometimes those bones rise up between them, then the patient should rest his foot on the ground prepared in this way (as with a splint).
Then place one foot on that which is raised from those bones, and press, bind, and hold it in place until it returns to its original position.
Then apply a plaster and stopper on top of it, and place under the foot a small table with two flat heads.
And strengthen it with the foot in the correct position, because you have wrapped it with cloth and other causes for which it is necessary.
And when three or four days have passed, then you remove the ligament because you will find the bones bound, or broken or dislocated, and the operation in these is done in the same way as we mentioned above.
However, if some toes are broken, then you restore them, bind them in the same way as I recommend to you in the restoration of fingers on the hand. Then place the broken toe splinter according to the length of the toe, and it should be slightly curved inward; and under the foot place this table that I have recommended to you, and strengthen it with correct positioning.
However, if the fracture of the bones is two or three or more, then you place a splint according to the size of each toe wrapped in linen cloth on each toe, and strengthen the table under the heel of the foot, and stand it on one of the heads of the table so that it holds well.
And it is important for you to remember that you do not forget all the causes mentioned to you in other fractures due to accidents we have mentioned; and oppose yourself to all accidents that are necessary.
Chapter XVIII.
On the fracture of the hump of the woman and the bone of the pubic arch and the buttock of the man.
When the hump of the woman is broken, you let it heal in a hanging position, and incline it to the opposite side slightly, and hold it behind.
And the midwife fills the hump with cotton, and it should be done in the hump in this way as expected; and the woman is shaken, and the hips are raised slightly, because all this is forgotten until it comes forward to the opposite side of the hump as expected and the broken bone returns.
Then place powder on the hips, and when it is time to urinate, remove the cottons with ease and urinate, then reduce and return it in the same way as you placed it at the beginning, and then return the powder first.
Make an opening in this manner for seven days or around that time, as it will be restored.
If you wish, take an ovine bladder and force a canal made of reed through it; insert the entire bladder into the cavity and inflate the canal with air until the bladder is distended within the cavity, as the fracture will return.
Then fill it with cotton and leave it in place for several days as prescribed until it heals.
And when the bones of the pelvis in males or females are fractured, administer in their restoration and treatment of the hip bone what has been prescribed for the bone of the pelvis.
That which is right should not be hidden in these nearby fractures and that do not require much effort, especially to one who has some knowledge, study, use, and understanding of this book; for that fracture indicates to you the greatest dispositions towards righteousness in their restoration and stricture, therefore understand well.
But in the case of the male's broken tailbone, take guarguamela root and place it on the fracture; then cover it with cloth and wrap it up, let it rest for three days or around that time, and it will heal.
Chapter XIX
Of restoration of bone fractures when infected.
It is appropriate for one who has suffered a fracture with infection, and especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or shoulder, or similar to it, that you cauterize and probe it as soon as the conditions for cauterization or probing are present as stated above.
And if the infection discharges pus, you must take measures to retain it until you pulverize gold dust over it, unless there is another reason.
Then begin the restoration of the fracture on that day, and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs cauterized, you must let the restoration wait for up to nine days until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth day and thereafter, as you will cause harm.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin exposed, you must perforate it and reduce the fracture with your hands with ease and minimal extension.
But if the extension is not prepared and the situation with your hands, you must reduce it with these tools; this tool is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and the width according to the size of the infection; and this is necessary for the physician who will take three or four according to the amount required in the treatment of all types of fractures.
And be round, and be slightly coarse in size to avoid the compression of the fracture during the operation.
And be of sharp edges with a margin at the ends; the upper end should be thicker and from the middle towards the fractures.
This is the form of it:
It is called "labre voluntary pallet" in Greek, so it is a small flat piece of bone.
Therefore the end of it must be placed slightly elevated above the end of the prominent bone.
Insert it once into a wound until the bone is reduced and set properly.
Therefore force the fracture ends together, and if the broken end is thin, do not use the tool for good reason, but you must insert the end of the bone until the tool can grip it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been prescribed, then insert it using what is necessary for the chisels which we have mentioned, or it can be with one of the saws in any way that is possible for you.
Then remove that part which remains rough and full of small splinters.
When the bone has been reduced and the patient experiences intense pain quickly, you must understand that the bone has not returned to its natural location; and thus do what will give great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete and you have placed a black stiptic cloth over it, do not apply any oil to the infection or other cause in which there is ointment, as this will cause putridity and corruption.
Approximately administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound exposed only as much as necessary, to incise it with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
Be careful not to stretch the wound with force, for many times ignorant physicians have caused death or severe illness or amputation in their patients, as this leads to healing. If the patient's wound is loose, soft and different from the strictures of other fractures.
If the wound is large or bad and you fear the risk of a bad outcome, and pain occurs at the site, it is not necessary to place stitches there, but rather apply a hard bandage in place of stitches and tighten it with that.
When the wound begins about one or two days later, which is caused by a blow, then remove the cloth that you had placed on it and apply warm compresses, as it is our custom to treat wounds, such as warm compresses like tetrapotassium and similar ones.
It is necessary for you to remove the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and it is important that the limb is prepared in such a way that the purulence flows from it to the joints nearby with ease.
If the wound persists for many days and is not consolidated or does not abscess from the blow, then you must realize that this is a complication of the small vessels, so examine the wound with care. If those complications that are separated and extracted do not occur, and those that are not separated and place the limb and cause pain, then you must incise them according to what is possible for you.
If the wound becomes gangrenous or rotten, this is a symptom or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to place a poultice on one of them according to what is appropriate for its healing, which is preceded by a narrative in its chapter.
And regarding what needs the most attention and you present your case: when large bones are broken, raise the limb in the same way as the bones of the thigh and similar ones of large members, without presuming traction or extraction of it, for many times this leads to death; leave it inside until it can be moved, for a strong limb may fall apart within two to thirty days. And you take care of the wound if you have seen it, and do not leave it untreated.
Chapter XX:
On the treatment of a node that occurs in the trace of any fracture.
This node often occurs in the trace of healing from a fracture, and more frequently near joints, and it is caused by the figure of the limb; strong limbs prohibit their natural operation.
Therefore consider whether the node is recent and administer medications that have astringent properties such as alum, tannins, and others; take some or all of these, and compress them with vi stiptic or album of huou or other astringents, and place it on the node in a cloth, and press it firmly around it for good compression.
Leave the extraction that does not loosen it for many days; and remove it after the node is gone; or press firmly on a lead sheet tightly, as lead has the property of holding everything that is inflated in the limbs.
But if the node has become hard and tight and necessitates removing it constantly, then you should cut it along the most superior part of it, and excise the excess and protruding portions or remove it with forceps until it is gone; and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter XXI:
In restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and remains limb subtle and free following its nature.
When a fracture of the bone is restored, and it remains limb subtle and free, it does not happen except for multiple causes: one of which is an excess of solution of the ligament and the ligature made not according to what is needed; or due to superfluity of tightness of the ligaments before nutriment is prohibited from running to the member; or due to excessive embrocaccio [a type of bandaging or splinting]; or due to movement superfluent not in its time; or due to small amount of blood in the patient's body and debility.
And the cure of this is to feed the patient and make their heart tender before the blood is multiplied in them, and administer baths, and give warm and sweet food and comfort over him/her.
Then place plaster on the limb, so that the plaster draws to it the nourishment, or continue the bandaging of him/her with warm water, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural shape.
Chapter XXII.
On the cure of broken bones when they are restored crookedly and the operation on them is forbidden what is necessary.
When a limb that has been restored appears to lack healing of its crookedness and prominence at the broken bone and nodule, and it is due to the shape of the member, but the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then you should take the word of the one who states that the limb was newly fractured.
But there are many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land, and this operation is greatly to be condemned as leading to great fear of the sign of which death is mortal.
But if the limpness and nodule are recent, then it is necessary that they be bandaged with warm water in which are placed soft herbs soothing, such as althea leaves and razor roots, and mellilot, and similar to these; and it should be made into a plaster with plaster of mollicificatio, or the mucillage of razor root althea is prepared and rubbed with the gray of gualina or oil of camphor, and it is plastered with that; or the figs are soaked in oil and crushed with the pomegranate seeds, and similar to these of the medicines called amermans consolidation.
