Chapter XXIII.
On the speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is essentially of one of the joints of its place, which is forbidden to move, and distorts the shape of the limb, and brings pain and great injuries upon the patient.
Therefore when an accident occurs with a dislocation, it is necessary to do so immediately and not delay the reduction, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes necrotic and difficult reduction of dislocation becomes very difficult.
And for this reason, it is not good nor stretched out at the time of the necrosis, because many times brings spasms and slow pain.
But when this occurs, it is necessary that you take the flap of the patient's skin, and let it be cauterized before the necrosis subsides; and the limb should be bandaged with warm water and oil, and the reduction should be made with ease.
And all the member should be healed by the means that will come in its place.
And you have now and ordered the chapters of dislocation as it was before in the fracture; from the upper division of the body to the lower part of it.
Chapter XXIV.
On the cure of dislocation of the jaw below.
If both jaws are dislocated and not straightened; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are brought back to their places with little movement and softened a bit; or they are completely dislocated and closed, until they are mollified at the juncture until the saliva of the patient flows and he/she cannot keep it nor close his/her mouth, and the tongue of him/her is enveloped in its socket.
But if the dislocation of it is slight, then with the greatest care it should be returned to its place by itself when there is little cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and closed, it is necessary that reduction of it be administered gently, and not be delayed at all.
And the attendant should hold the head of the patient, and enter his/her thumb into the root of the dislocation of the jaw within the mouth if the dislocation is of one part; or that he/she enters both thumbs together, if the dislocation is of two parts, and the others should be outside with which he/she guides it; and commands the patient to cease his/her jaws and release them from going in any direction; and the doctor should guide the jaw and cover the jaw with a piece of cloth until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially when both mandibles are dislocated, then administer an emulsified solution of hot water and oil until it becomes easy to reduce both, and do not delay the reduction of both for too long, as we have said.
And when they have been returned and closed, the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place it on those small pieces of woolen cloth with chalk, make it from beeswax and rubbed oil; and apply it gently to facilitate and loosen the closing.
The patient should sleep on their back, with the head placed between two pillows to prevent it from turning to either side; do not force any cause of nausea upon them; when their pain is reduced and the mandibles are in place, then you may move them as desired.
This should be administered gently and not force the opening of the mouth; you must maintain their food and drink, and rest until the mandible is set and healed.
If the reduction of both mandibles, when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, is difficult, then observe carefully for fevers and constant sweating, and it is strong to provoke vomiting with pure bile.
And when you see this, you should know it is a disaster; and carefully observe that the one to whom this happens will die within ten days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the end of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated from the inside through its continuity with the breastbone; and sometimes it is dislocated on the outside, which can be determined by examination.
The restoration of this is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. Then compress the area with your hand with strong compression, for it will return.
And place a plaster and small pieces of cloth over it, and firmly apply pressure to that area.
However, if the end of that which is connected to it is not dislocated at any time, it should be reduced and closed as we have said and as you are equipped.
And place that plaster, small pieces of cloth and bandage over it, and command the patient to rest in quiet until healed.
This method of treatment reduces the end of the muscle when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of a dislocation in the muscle.
Know that a muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the armpit to the lower side.
And the second is that it is dislocated above the muscle, but this is rare; and it is not dislocated at the back of the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
Furthermore, it is dislocated and protrudes at the lower sides of the armpits, especially in those with little flesh, it is easily dislocated; but in those with much flesh, it protrudes outwardly with difficulty.
And a strong blow or fall can cause it to occur in some men, and the muscle of that person is cut off by cauterization, and treated with what has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you should examine this carefully before proceeding with treatment.
The dislocation of this is known when it occurs at the lower sides of the armpits; therefore you should distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the undamaged one, for there are clear signs of difference; and examine the head of the muscle for a concavity on top and the touch of its head at the end of the armpit like a hump, and when the patient cannot reduce their hand to their elbow or move it at all.
If it is dislocated toward the breastbone or inwardly, you should examine it carefully and not be misled.
The reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
The reduction of this is that the attendant raises the hand to the upper sides, and holds their hands at the lower end of the dislocation, and raises the joint upward with strength to its place, and the attendant raises the hand, and extends it upwards, and holds it there because it returns easily.
However, if it does not return by the method we have described, and the dislocation has just occurred, wait several days, and then the patient should be bathed in hot water and embrocation made of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and administered.
And the patient should be placed on their back and held at the lower end of the dislocation with a cloth that is neither too loose nor too tight, and the doctor should place his foot on the shoulder, lift the head of the muscle upward, pull the hand of the patient toward the upper sides so it is not displaced again, and another attendant should hold the head of the patient, who should not be displaced toward the lower sides, as it will soon return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce [the hernia] in this manner: it is that you make a man present who is taller than the patient, and he stands on the side of the affected one, enters his muscle underneath and tickles the patient's hernia, lifting it towards the incision above until the patient is suspended in the air, and another servant pulls the patient's hand from below his abdomen. If the patient is lightweight, then it is necessary that the one who causes it be suspended, so that gravity helps, because the dislocation returns quickly.
It is reduced in the same way by another method: it is that a long pole with its head rounded like the shape of a mortar pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, is placed next to the patient; then, fabric cloth lions are placed on top of the pole's head, and the patient stands according to the length of the pole, with his hand extended on one side and the other person extends his body from the opposite side with force, because the joint returns quickly to its place.
If the reduction is difficult due to all the reasons mentioned earlier, then use this cure: it is that you take a pole whose length is two forearms and its width four fingers, and its thickness is relieved at the end of the tip in this form:
Fabric cloth lions are placed over the rounded head to avoid hurting the patient.
The tip is placed next to the patient's hernia and his hand or arm is extended over the pole towards the opposite side, and the pole is tied with ropes to the helper and the end of the hand.
The arm is placed on a step of stairs according to its width, and the hand is extended towards the opposite side, while the other body is suspended from the other side, because the joint will return quickly.
Once the intestine is inserted as completely as possible in any manner that is available to you, then it is necessary that you place a linen bandage around the hernia; and apply a plaster prepared with powder of milled alum on top of the muscle, so that it is rolled over, and apply strong pressure below the hernia with a strap; and the ligament is wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hand is connected to his neck, and it is left, and no damage should be done to the patient's hand for 7 days.
It is necessary that the food of the patient be reduced, until the member is comfortable, because it helps the healing process more quickly.
And give less food for 7 or 5 days and exercise movement; and if it is closed and not softened, then it is healed.
If the joint is dislocated several times due to humidity that occurs in it or for some other reason, then in that case, cautery with three strokes should be administered, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all of this is done and the ligament is removed after 7 days and the joint is not closed, and the plaster and strap are reduced on it several times, but it is still not closed, and the catheter cannot raise the arm above the incision, then you know that the nerves of it, which are at the head of the muscle, have already been severed or extended or softened, and you should understand that the joint will never be able to return to its place.
The dislocation of that one is caused by the feet and the breast or the hips behind it, the reduction is done with a push and extension to the maximum until it returns.
Another strap should be administered in that one, and care for it until it heals.
If there is gravity or delay in the member's healing or movement, then the patient should use hot baths several times until the gravity is relieved and it returns to its original nature.
Chapter XXVII.
On the treatment of shoulder dislocation.
You should know that the shoulder joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; it can be dislocated in all directions, but most commonly in front of the back, and the dislocation of it is not relieved by any position or touch according to a certain shape, and when you adjust the displaced shoulder correctly, it is clearly manifested that the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch it with its own muscle.
And you must catch it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes an abscess cauterized, because if it reaches a cauterized abscess, reducing it is difficult, and recovery is incomplete; moreover, when it occurs at the back, it is worse for all other types of dislocations, and more painful, and more often leads to death.
The restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the hand of it is extended with both arms, and the arm is stretched out, and the hands of the doctor are on the shoulder and below it, and he pushes the joint with his fingers or the blade of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in front, it returns by bending the arm with its own hand until it hits the shoulder with the blade of the palm.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and strong motion.
And that two servants extend the arms, and let them have the same position so that there is no resistance to the extension.
Approach the arm should be rolled up entirely, as it is wrapped in long doubled cloth around its hand or bandaged.
When the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil to help alleviate the cause of pain and increase lubrication in the joint, then approach and push the joint firmly until it returns.
Then the reduction should be stopped on that plaster where there is stiffness and hardness with albumen of egg, and it should be extracted with a descending force, the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left for some days.
Then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you remove the bandage from there and leave it.
If the joint is not strong, you should loosen the plaster and bandage, leave it for some days until it is extracted, then remove it.
But if there is gravity in the reduction of the joint and its position is moving, administer humectation in bathing and gentle friction, and compression, until it is alleviated; or rest on the joint for a few days, so that it can heal itself; then tie it up and leave it on the bones for the day and night. Then remove it and put it in the bath; when it becomes warm, you should massage the joint with tempered friction, and then re-apply it, which is a movement of the roller, once, twice, and thrice, Corruption has occurred inside the denouement, until it is limited.
And if you want, place it on the soft and scalded cowhide wound with sewing and press it; and do this several times, as it is wet and reduces itself to its position.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder joint, which is between the arm and the hand.
The shoulder joint of the hand is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of the dislocation is easy, but on the contrary for other joints, however it requires speed in the reduction at the time of dislocation, before it becomes fixed or a cauterization occurs at the site.
The reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table and extend their arm, then place your hand or palm over the edge of the joint, and push it back into its place.
However, it is important to consider whether the palm has already been left in the center of the hand, in which case you should place the patient's hands on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is preceded by this, then you should place the patient's hand in the intrinsic position on the table, so that the doctor's hand fits over the edge of the joint.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; otherwise, press it with a plaster allowing the site to heal and leave it; do not reapply it, as otherwise, it will cause pain or you may not be able to work on the reduction for some days.
However, the joint returns according to its curvature; and do not tell the patient any cause, but let their hand become softened and unable to strengthen any cause; then know that the nerves are broken or crushed, and do not be cautious in this matter except when it is strengthened with a strong cautery for the healing of the site.
When the shoulder is returned, press it with the plaster that has been recommended, and let it be left and strengthened for 5 days; then remove it and let the patient rest and exercise their arm.
However, if the movement of the shoulder remains and there is gravity in it, you should soften it with hot water and massage it for several times until it is alleviated.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various parts.
And when a finger of those is dislocated at the base or center of it, you should squeeze the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the finger's head, and suspend the part to which it is dislocated, and let it rest for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it straightens on that same day.
And when it is night, let it rest according to the narrative, and do not cease to remove it during the day and exercise it with movement, and leave it at night; and do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same for a dislocation of the wrist, and place it in that position, and perform the same first operation until it heals.
And similarly, do as you would for when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae in the back.
When a spine or neck vertebrae becomes dislocated and other vertebrae are also displaced along with it, that is not treatable because death awaits the patient.
A sign of this condition is that the patient's excretion is outside their will and they cannot retain it; and in many cases, some limbs or legs or arms, or one of them are swollen or affected by paralysis, or a vertebra has been dislocated from its place. In many cases, this means that the vertebra has moved and the patient is experiencing paralysis in four different areas.
Therefore, it has been dislocated in the rear area and is called sciatica. (This line appears to be an interpretation or explanation of the condition discussed earlier.)
However, the treatment of this condition is to consider whether sciatica occurs in childhood because in such cases, there is neither cure nor recovery for it. (This line appears to be a continuation of the previous explanation or interpretation.)
But in cases that occur from an accident or blow, or similar to those, the Ancients have described many ways of treatment at length, but I shall summarize it here as succinctly as possible, for brevity's sake; nevertheless, I have abbreviated this treatment method by omitting much of the detail, using more straightforward language to make the explanation clearer. With that said, I will now describe and elaborate on the instruments used in this method.
Only that type of sciatica is treated which occurs in the back, as I will recommend. (This line appears to be a conclusion or summary of the discussion about treatment for sciatica.)
The patient should be laid flat on their back and propped up with cushions or pillows to prevent the feet from touching the ground. (This line appears to describe a position for the patient during treatment of sciatica.)
A straight stick should be placed beneath the head at its foot end, and another stick under the feet at the other end of the bed. (This line appears to describe additional equipment or positioning for the patient during treatment of sciatica.)
The caregiver should hold the stick, and neither should it be fixed in place; another caregiver should hold the other according to this positioning. (This line appears to describe the roles of the attendants during treatment of sciatica.)
Cover the patient's head with a cloth and tuck it in securely to keep it from falling; extend the end of the bandage to the stick that supports the head, and tie it there; and tightly bind the other bandage around the patient's waist, covering their lower back, and make sure the sheets are straight; and adjust all the bandages so they are tied together to the stick that supports the feet. (This line appears to describe further details for positioning and securing the patient during treatment of sciatica.)
Approach the minister and extend the ligament to the division of the thigh, but do not let the two thighs and the ligaments in their places where they are inserted remain unbound, but rather loosen them slightly, but not as firmly as has been said.
The doctor places his hands on the spine with strength until it returns (or he places a board over that area and adjusts the patient's feet onto the board until it returns).
However, if it does not return in this way, then take a board whose length or width is three cubits, and carve into the wall near the patient a hole in which to insert the end of the board. Then the thickness of the board is placed on the spine and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and squeezes it tightly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
If you wish, turn the patient's head to the side; and you should place your feet behind his head at a distance of one forearm, and the distance between each foot should be a palm, and make a hole in each foot, firmly planting them on the ground so that they are not injured.
A fourth foot should be placed in a circle where the two feet meet.
And at the end of it is a hole where the foot is placed, its length being one palm around what is turned (or curved); and in the other feet, it is similar to that.
Then tighten the ligaments that you have tied around the patient's feet at the hole where his head should be, and the ligaments that you have tied around his thighs at the circle where his legs should be.
You must place each turn and have a minister who rotates the turn with his hand, and the doctor examines the spine as has been said.
When the spine has returned and the hole is closed, it is necessary to place a medicated plaster with hog's lard and stopper over it, and then place a board of the size of three fingers or slightly larger over the spot where the swelling is, and the length should be that which covers the swelling, and on top of the division of the spine.
And tie a linen cloth with the ligaments that we have mentioned, and use the patient for subtle nourishment until he is cured.
And if there remains any obstruction in the spot at the end of healing, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and smooth with the long-term administration of the board, which we have recommended; and already a sheet of lead has been applied to that spot.
Sometimes there occurs obstruction in the lower back of the two (spines), and it is caused by dislocation; and that bone that protrudes should not be approached with this medicine because it will cause you to die quickly.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated pelvis.
Know that the joint of the pelvis and the joint of the muscles do not move except for dislocation alone, and it does not happen at those joints as it happens with other small displacements and depressions.
And the joint of the pelvis is dislocated in four ways: it can be dislocated at the internal divisions, or at the external divisions, or at the front divisions, or at the back divisions.
Moreover, it is most often dislocated at the internal divisions, and rarely at the front or back divisions.
The sign of the dislocation at the internal divisions is that when you adjust the healthy thigh with the sick one, the healthy thigh is longer and the sick leg is more prominent than the healthy one, and the sick person cannot bend his knee; and the location where the thigh should be joined has been marked with a cauterized apostema, so that the head of the thigh has already reached this point.
The sign of the dislocation at the external divisions is that the accidents at that location are opposite to this accident.
The sign of the dislocation at the front divisions is that the thigh spreads out in compliance, but does not bend without pain in the front, and if it is forced to walk and cannot advance towards the front; and the urine is retained and the opening of the bladder is cauterized, and you should have the patient walk on crutches.
The dislocation of the part (hip) in that person can be recognized by the fact that the thigh does not expand, nor can it bend the affected limb as it doubles into the socket, and the socket is shorter than the other, and the hip is softened, and the head of the femur should be directed towards that location.
However, the reduction of that type of dislocation you consider, if the dislocation is old and chronic, it has already been accustomed to its position, in which case, you do not need to perform a reduction; therefore, it is not a complete cure; and therefore, it is not necessary for progression (of treatment).
If the dislocation is recent and one of the four types of dislocations, then you must aim at returning the joint and extend it between the thighs inside and outside, to the left and right, so that it returns easily without needing other treatment.
If it does not return in this manner, you must then prepare a strong tool and extend the socket of the thigh lower down, or use a rope that is tied above the hip; and another person extends their hand above in such a way as to place their hands deeper into the hip joint; and hold tight with the rope near the base of the femur, and have another person keep the end of the rope taut.
The extension is made from the thigh to the hip joint at the anterior part of the neck or from the back part of the thigh to the back part of the chest.
The extension should be performed in one continuous movement, with the patient raised off the ground and hanging down.
This type of extension is common to all four types.
Therefore, if the dislocation returns in the manner described, it will be good; otherwise, it is necessary for all types of dislocations to do what I have said about their own treatment.
In the reduction of this type, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is inside the thighs, the patient should be placed on their back; and the ropes are stopped at the base of the femur in the area between the head of the femur and the lower neck of the hip; and then extend the rope from the front part of the hip to the more anterior part of the chest.
And another strong person takes hold of the arms, and grasps the large part of the diseased thigh and extends it outwards by forceful extension, as it returns to its place.
And this type is easier to cure than other types for which this member can be reduced.
However, if the patient is too weak to comply with this type of treatment, it is necessary to tie both their feet together with a strong, light rope around their ankles and thighs.
And let the length of each be four fingers, and the diseased thigh should be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And the patient is suspended over the head of the bed, and it should be elevated as much as possible; and the distance from the ground to the patient should be two forearms.
And command a strong young person to press the head of the femur, and suspend the patient from another young person, and have another young person push on the hip with force, as the joint returns to its place quickly.
However, in the reduction of this type, when it is dislocated outside the thighs, the patient should walk on crutches as recommended earlier; and the rope is tightened around the hip of the diseased person and their feet.
And two beds should be placed, one under the feet and the other under the head; and two more beds adjusted to the middle of the pole fixed over which damp cloths are wrapped around it, so that the patient is not damaged by the pole being between their legs, so that the extension at the back should not be obstructed.
And each person extends to their part, and the doctor with his hand guides the reduction according to art.
And when the dislocation is in front of the thighs, it is necessary to gently extend the socket and according to the story mentioned earlier on the pole; and the doctor places his right palm over the neck of the hip, and then compresses it with the other hand, and this compression stops the extended hip at the front part of the thigh near the groin.
And if the dislocation is behind the thighs, it is not necessary to extend the patient's leg backwards and elevate it above the ground; instead, it must be placed on a hard surface as if the ankle of the foot is dislocated outwards, as described earlier in the treatment for that purpose, and it should lie on its side and the ropes should be extended as described, but there will be no benefit.
It is necessary to administer compression with a table at the joint location.
Therefore, when the reduction is difficult as required - and the sign of its difficulty is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend two blankets for the patient, and when you see the patient's thighs narrowing and the patient stretches their own leg and expands it without excuse, then you know that the member requires reduction - and then you adjust two cushions and wait with plaster, pulling gently so as not to harm any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the linens and plaster, and compare one blanket with another.
If you see the thighs returning to their normal position, then know that the dislocation is closed, and leave the patient to move freely.
If you see any signs of swelling, then return and bandage it, and pull it gently according to the first structure, and leave it for three days.
Then loosen that, and make it walk slowly for several days, until it is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder.
The shoulder can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated at the exterior joints, the internal joints, and more often at the posterior joints; but not at the anterior joint.
The sign of dislocation is when you ask the patient to apply their arm to their chest, and then you know that the shoulder is dislocated.
The restoration of all the types of dislocation is to place the patient straight, so that they can extend their arm if possible; hold your hands on the side of it, and press the foot of it between your feet; and bring the shoulder towards your palms, and keep those in position on the shoulder until it returns to its place. And another servant should extend their own leg into the shoulder of the patient until the shoulder returns to its place.
The sign of reduction is when the arm adheres to the chest without laboring.
Then bandage it and make the arm adhere to the chest, and tie them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then loosen the bandages.
Do not let the patient walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of it is excused, then administer strong extension with linens as described in the treatment of hernia in the previous narrative.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes reduced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated due to injury, and the dislocation of it can occur inwardly or outwardly.
The sign of dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen on the side where it is dislocated.
But the treatment of reduction is gentle, and it should be extended easily and then retracted until it returns to its place.
But in the treatment of a dislocation caused by injury, it is necessary to place the patient straight, and have a strong and able servant on the side of the back of that in the right position.
Place your right hand on the top of the patient's foot and your left hand on the part below the foot at the location of the heel.
Push the foot towards you with your right hand, place your left hand on it twice and stretch it, then push the foot's leg towards the bed with your right hand.
Do this twice as you have counted.
Push the foot's leg to the bed on the third count, and you are at the heel.
If the foot returns after one or two counts using this method, that is good; if not, continue the procedure, for the foot will return again.
But if reducing it using the method that we have recommended is prohibited, then make the patient lie down on their back on solid ground, stretch out a piece of cloth on the ground and have them lie between it, and you have already wrapped them in cloth to avoid injuring the patient.
Have the minister (assistant) hold their (the patient's) legs, and have another minister extend the foot with their hands or place it on a pillow so that it rests on the neck of the foot; and have each minister extend that part of the body in the opposite direction, while the cloth remains between the patient's legs to prevent the body from being pulled apart at the sides beyond its extension.
And the doctor straightens (reduces) the dislocation with their hands, and another minister holds the healthy limb at the sides, for the dislocation will return easily.
And so when the dislocation returns and the reduction is confirmed to you, then stop applying the plaster and remove it firmly, and tie the foot to the bed.
It is necessary to be careful or protect the nerves that are behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not too tight and does not hurt the area too much.
Leave it like that for two or three days; if the bandage becomes soaked, remove it.
Remove the bandage on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from walking for forty days. But if walking in front of that space is necessary, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be resolved and destroyed, and this would not receive treatment.
If an abscess is discovered, it is necessary to provide care in the manner described earlier (in the procedure), at another location for treatment and dressing, until it has drained completely.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocation of toes in feet.
It is necessary that one who is dislocated be reduced gently with slight extension; and if it is not difficult, it should be done easily.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the second toe (the two smaller toes), then it is necessary to restrain the patient's foot on a level surface, such as the ground or a table, and keep it still as if he were walking.
Then stand by the patient's foot, and place it on something that supports the joints, and press it with your foot firmly until it returns to its original position, and make sure it remains stable and does not appear in the affected area again.
Then place the patient's leg on a table that holds the entire foot, with two ends, and press it firmly with a structure of appropriate strength and stability for three days; then loosen it and let it rest from walking for several days until it is strengthened and the recovery is assured.
Chapter 35.
On the types of dislocation caused by wound, fracture, or any other conditions simultaneously.
When some cause of these occurs, and you engage in the treatment and restoration of it, many times death follows.
Therefore, it is not necessary to approach the treatment presumptuously, but one who is skilled in the art, with long experience, mercy, and compassion, should proceed slowly, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and use remedies that stop pus cauterized only when necessary, leave the patient to divine judgment, but help those for whom you hope salvation from destruction and alleviation of illness, and in which there is some hope. And thus strive to reduce it at the beginning of the cause before pus forms a cauterization.
And if the member returns as we wish, then administer treatment that stops pus cauterization and heals the wound with what is appropriate for dry dressings.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, you should perform the treatment for their setting, and do what has been said in simple illnesses before these locations.
And be careful and cautious with the instrument in the paths of fear as you have been taught by my preaching; because it is better for you to follow your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthy to your true body those things at your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis may God be praised. Amen.
